  • If the program requires multi-year funding, what is the total projected cost over its lifecycle?
  • Has the agency prepared an Indicative Annual Procurement Plan for this program?
  • Has the program received BPDA certification for alignment with the BDP?
  • Has the program received endorsement from the relevant Budget Endorsing Authority (MPW for infrastructure, MENRE for environment, ICO for ICT, BWC for GAD)?
  • If this is an expansion of an existing program, what evidence shows the existing program is performing well enough to justify scaling up?

Overall Budget

  • What is the total budget request compared to the previous year’s allocation? What is the percentage change?
  • What proportion of the budget increase is driven by PS versus MOOE versus CO?
  • If the Parliament approves a reduced allocation — say, 90 percent of the request — which programs would the agency cut or defer?
  • What are the agency’s three highest-priority budget items? What are the three lowest?

These questions are starting points. Tailor them based on the specific agency’s budget data. The most effective budget scrutiny questions are not generic — they reference specific line items, specific programs, and specific numbers from the agency’s own submission.

Sequencing Your Questions

The order of questions matters. In a budget hearing, you typically have limited time. Agency heads and budget officers are skilled at consuming time with long answers to open-ended questions. Structure your questions to get the most important information first.

Start with performance questions — did the agency deliver what it promised? Then move to variance questions — why did the numbers change? Then move to new program questions — why should the Parliament fund something new? End with accountability questions — what are the agency’s specific commitments for the coming fiscal year?

This sequence builds a narrative. Performance questions establish the baseline. Variance questions identify the gaps. New program questions test the agency’s ambitions against its track record. Accountability questions lock in commitments that can be monitored in the next budget cycle.

A practical tip: draft the questions on individual cards or separate pages. During the hearing, the MP can shuffle the order based on how the proceeding unfolds. If another MP asks one of your prepared questions, the MP can skip it and move to the next. If the agency’s answer to one question opens a new line of inquiry, the MP can insert a follow-up without losing the overall structure.

9.9 Recommendations for the MP

The budget briefer concludes with your recommendations. As with the legislative briefer, the staff prepares the analysis and recommendations; the MP makes the decision.

Structure your recommendations clearly:

Recommended Position on the Overall Budget

State whether you recommend that the MP support, conditionally support, or oppose the agency’s overall budget request. If conditional, specify the conditions — adoption of certain amendments, receipt of additional documentation, or resolution of specific concerns.

Recommended Amendments to the Budget

If your analysis reveals problems — unjustified increases, unfunded mandates, non-performing programs, or misaligned priorities — recommend specific budget amendments. These can take the form of:

  • Reduction. Reduce a specific line item by a stated amount. (e.g., “Reduce the CO allocation for Project X by PhP 50 million, pending completion of the feasibility study.”)
  • Reallocation. Transfer funds from one line item to another within the same agency. (e.g., “Reallocate PhP 20 million from administrative MOOE to the scholarship program under Program B.”)
  • Conditional release. Recommend that a specific allocation be approved but released only upon compliance with stated conditions. (e.g., “Approve the Tier 2 allocation for Program D, subject to submission of a detailed implementation plan within 90 days.”)
  • Deletion. Recommend the removal of a line item entirely. This is rare and should be reserved for proposals that are clearly unjustified.

For each recommended amendment, provide a brief justification — the same one-paragraph structure used in the legislative briefer’s amendment section.

A note on budget amendments: unlike legislative amendments, which change the text of a bill, budget amendments change numbers. The amendment form is different. Instead of “delete the words ‘’ and insert ‘,’” a budget amendment states: “Reduce the allocation for [agency] under [program/expenditure item] from PhP ___ to PhP .” Or: “Insert a new line item under [program] for [purpose] in the amount of PhP .” When reducing one item and increasing another, the MP should present both sides of the reallocation together so the body can see that the total budget remains balanced.

The rules on budget amendments vary — some Parliaments require that any increase in one line item be offset by a decrease in another (revenue-neutral amendments). Others allow amendments that increase total spending. Check the Parliament’s Rules of Procedure and the Committee on Finance’s guidelines for the specific rules applicable in the current budget cycle.

Recommended Questions for the Hearing

From the full list of scrutiny questions in Section 9.8, select the five to ten most important questions for this agency. Rank them by priority. If the hearing runs short, the MP should ask these questions first.

Items Requiring Follow-Up

List any items that cannot be resolved during the budget hearing and require follow-up — missing data, documents the agency promised to submit, issues deferred to plenary deliberation. These items should be tracked in the action log.

Action Log and Approval

Use the same action log and approval block format as the legislative briefer:

Date Action Status
[date] BEP submitted to Parliament Complete
[date] Agency budget data compiled Complete
[date] Budget briefer prepared Complete
[date] Briefer submitted to MP for review Pending
[date] Committee on Finance hearing Upcoming — [date]
[date] Plenary budget deliberation TBD

The approval block:

  • Prepared by: [Staff member name and designation]
  • Date submitted: [Date]
  • Recommended position: [Support / Support with amendments / Oppose / Reserve position]

For the MP:

  • Approved as recommended: ☐
  • Approved with modifications: ☐
  • Disapproved / Return for further study: ☐
  • MP’s signature: ___
  • Date: ___

The Budget as a Policy Document

Budget work is sometimes treated as the dull sibling of legislative drafting. It lacks the drama of interpellation, the intellectual satisfaction of crafting a new law, the visibility of authoring a landmark bill. But budget scrutiny is where the Parliament’s oversight function is most consequential.

Every peso in the Bangsamoro Expenditure Program is a decision. The decision to fund a road in one province is simultaneously a decision not to fund a road in another. The decision to create fifty new positions in one ministry is a decision to leave fifty positions unfunded elsewhere. Budget scrutiny forces those trade-offs into the open.

The budget briefer is the instrument that enables your MP to make those trade-offs intelligently. It converts raw financial data into actionable analysis. It connects spending to outcomes. It identifies waste, misalignment, and missed opportunities. And it ensures that the MP walks into the budget deliberation with the same level of preparation they would bring to a major policy bill — because the budget is a major policy document. It is arguably the most important one the Parliament passes each year.

The six development goals of the BDP 2023-2028 are not just aspirational statements. They represent the Bangsamoro people’s priorities as adopted by the Parliament. Goal 1 demands accountable government. Goal 2 demands an equitable economy. Goal 3 demands peaceful communities. Goal 4 demands quality social services. Goal 5 demands cultural preservation. Goal 6 demands resilient infrastructure. Every budget line item should advance at least one of these goals. When a line item does not, the MP should ask why.

That is the work. It requires patience, numerical literacy, and a willingness to dig into the details. But it is some of the most important work a legislative office can do.

The fiscal sources that fund the BEP — the annual block grant from the national government, the Special Development Fund, locally generated revenues, and other sources enumerated in the BOL — are finite. Every peso allocated to one purpose is a peso unavailable for another. The budget briefer makes those trade-offs visible. It puts numbers on choices. It connects spending to performance. It links appropriations to development goals.

Prepare the briefer. Do the analysis. Give the MP everything they need.

That is Completed Staff Work applied to the budget, and it is the standard your office should meet every fiscal year.

Back to Table of Contents

Chapter 10 — Amendments

A bill does not always arrive at the plenary in its final form. More often, it arrives in a form that needs work. Committee members spot problems during scrutiny. Floor deliberations reveal gaps. A Member raises a concern during interpellation that no one anticipated. The response to all of these is the amendment.

Amendments are how the Parliament refines legislation. They are the mechanism by which a draft bill — sometimes rough, sometimes incomplete, sometimes overtaken by new information — becomes a law that the Parliament as a whole can support. If bill drafting is architecture, amendments are the renovation work. They insert rooms that were missing, remove walls that block the light, and replace materials that turned out to be unsuitable.

For the drafter, amendments represent some of the most demanding work in the legislative process. You must draft language that fits seamlessly into an existing text — matching its style, numbering, and terminology — while achieving a specific change that the sponsoring MP or committee has requested. You must do this quickly, often during a session, sometimes while the debate is still underway. And you must do it precisely, because a poorly drafted amendment can create more problems than the defect it was meant to fix.

This chapter covers the types of amendments recognized in the Bangsamoro Parliament, the procedures that govern them, and the exact language and format you should use when drafting them. It draws on the Rules, Procedures, and Practices of the Bangsamoro Transition Authority Parliament — Resolution No. 268 — as the governing procedural framework.

10.1 Types of Amendments

Resolution No. 268 recognizes two broad categories of amendments that can be presented during plenary deliberation:

Committee Amendments. These are alterations or changes — by addition, deletion, substitution, or any combination — offered by the committee that considered the bill. Committee amendments arise from the committee’s scrutiny of the bill during the committee stage. They reflect the committee’s collective judgment about what the bill needs.

Floor Amendments (Individual Amendments). These are alterations or changes offered by an individual Member of Parliament during the period of amendments in the plenary. Floor amendments reflect a particular Member’s view that the bill, even as modified by the committee, still needs further adjustment.

Both types follow the same basic mechanics. An amendment proposes to change the text of a bill in one or more of the following ways:

  • Insertion — adding new language where none existed before. This could be a new word, a new phrase, a new subsection, a new section, or even an entirely new article.
  • Deletion — removing existing language from the bill. This could be a single word, a phrase, a subsection, a section, or an entire article.
  • Substitution — replacing existing language with new language. This combines deletion and insertion into a single operation: you take out the old text and put in the new text.

These three operations — insertion, deletion, and substitution — are the building blocks of all amendment drafting. Every amendment, no matter how complex, reduces to one or some combination of these operations.

There is a fourth category that deserves separate treatment: the substitute bill. A substitute bill replaces the entire text of the original bill with a new version. It is, in effect, a complete rewrite offered as an amendment. Substitute bills follow special procedures and raise particular drafting challenges, which Section 10.5 addresses.

Important Constraints

Resolution No. 268 imposes several rules on amendments that every drafter must know:

Germaneness. An amendment that seeks to include a provision not germane to the purpose of the bill — called a “rider” — is not allowed. The one-subject rule applies to amendments just as it applies to bills. If a bill deals with fisheries regulation, you cannot amend it to include provisions about road construction. The amendment must relate to the subject matter of the bill being amended.

Specificity. Amendments must be “presented concretely and specifically in the course of the debate, indicating therein the page and the line in which the amendments are proposed to be made.” This is a procedural requirement, but it also imposes a drafting obligation. You cannot offer a vague amendment — “improve the penalty provision” — and leave it to the plenary to figure out the language. You must state exactly what text you propose to insert, delete, or substitute, and where.

Writing requirement for substitution amendments. “No amendment by substitution shall be entertained unless the text thereof is submitted in writing.” This means the drafter must prepare a written text before the amendment can be formally moved on the floor.

Withdrawal. Any amendment may be withdrawn before a vote is taken on it. Once the plenary votes, the amendment stands or falls.

Preservation of deleted language. Whenever a Member wishes to retain words or phrases that the committee deleted, that Member must present an amendment as if those words or phrases had never appeared in the bill. In other words, the amendment must affirmatively propose inserting the language back — it cannot simply argue that the committee’s deletion was wrong.

10.2 Committee Amendments

Committee amendments emerge during the committee stage of the legislative process. After a bill is referred to a committee following Second Reading, the committee examines it in detail — line by line, section by section. During this scrutiny, the committee may decide that certain provisions need to be changed, added, or removed.

How Committee Amendments Arise

The committee stage is where most of the substantive work on a bill takes place. The committee holds hearings, invites resource persons, consults stakeholders, and deliberates among its members. Through this process, the committee develops a set of proposed changes to the original bill.

These changes might reflect:

  • Technical corrections identified during hearings (a deadline that is impractical, a definition that is too narrow or too broad)
  • Substantive policy adjustments based on testimony from implementing agencies or affected communities
  • Legal issues flagged by the Policy Research and Legal Services (PRLS) or the committee’s own legal staff
  • Compromises reached among committee members with different views on a provision
  • Alignment issues with existing BAAs or national law that became apparent during scrutiny

The committee incorporates these changes into the bill and produces a committee report. The committee report contains the revised text of the bill — including all committee amendments — along with the discussions, justifications, and the votes of the members who participated in the final committee vote.

Procedure in Plenary

When the bill comes to the plenary for the Consideration and Deliberation Stage, committee amendments are taken up first, before any floor amendments. The procedure works as follows:

  1. The sponsor of the committee report presents the bill to the plenary, explaining what the committee did and why.
  2. After the period of interpellation, the period of amendments begins.
  3. The sponsor presents the committee amendments to the plenary.
  4. If the proponent (the original author of the bill) accepts the committee amendments, they are adopted without further debate.
  5. If the proponent does not accept the committee amendments, the period of amendment continues, and the proponent may propose alternative amendments.
  6. Committee amendments that are contested are debated and voted upon.

Drafting Committee Amendments

When you draft committee amendments for a committee report, present them in a way that makes the changes immediately clear. The committee report should show what the original bill said and what the committee version says. There are two common formats:

Narrative format. Describe each amendment in prose:

The Committee proposes the following amendments to the bill:

  1. Section 3(a) — The phrase “within thirty (30) days” is amended to read “within sixty (60) days” to allow sufficient time for the implementing agency to complete the required consultations.

  2. Section 7 — A new subsection (d) is inserted to read as follows: “(d) Coordination with local government units. — The Ministry shall coordinate with the local government units in the implementation of the programs under this Act.”

  3. Section 12 — The section is deleted in its entirety. The Committee found that the penal provision was duplicative of existing penalties under BAA No. 18.

Comparative matrix format. Present a two-column or three-column table showing the original provision and the committee version side by side. This format is more work to prepare but far easier for Members to follow during deliberation:

Original Bill Committee Amendment
SEC. 5. Eligibility. — Any person who has been a resident of the Bangsamoro for at least one (1) year… SEC. 5. Eligibility. — Any person who has been a resident of the Bangsamoro for at least three (3) years…
SEC. 10. Penalties. — Any person who violates this Act shall be punished by a fine of not less than Ten Thousand Pesos (PhP10,000.00)… SEC. 10. Penalties. — Any person who violates this Act shall be punished by a fine of not less than Fifty Thousand Pesos (PhP50,000.00)…

When you prepare committee amendments, bold or underline the changed text so that the changes are visible at a glance. This is not a legal requirement, but it is good practice that will save time during plenary deliberation.

10.3 Floor Amendments

Floor amendments — also called individual amendments — are proposed by Members of Parliament during the period of amendments in the plenary. Unlike committee amendments, which reflect a collective decision by the committee, floor amendments represent an individual Member’s view that the bill still needs revision.

When Floor Amendments Are Offered

Floor amendments are offered after committee amendments have been disposed of. The sequence during the Consideration and Deliberation Stage is:

  1. Committee report is presented
  2. Period of interpellation
  3. Period of amendments:
  4. a. Committee amendments (presented and voted on first)
  5. b. Floor amendments (presented and voted on after committee amendments)
  6. Voting on Second Reading

A Member who wishes to propose a floor amendment registers with the Majority Floor Leader, who maintains a list of Members proposing amendments. The Member is then recognized by the Speaker to present the amendment.

Procedure for Proposing a Floor Amendment

When a Member is recognized to propose a floor amendment, the process typically unfolds as follows:

  1. The Member states the specific provision to be amended, identifying the page, line, section, and subsection.
  2. The Member reads or describes the proposed amendment.
  3. The sponsor of the bill responds — accepting, rejecting, or proposing a modification of the amendment.
  4. If the amendment is accepted by the sponsor, it may be adopted without further debate.
  5. If the amendment is rejected by the sponsor, the plenary debates the amendment.
  6. The plenary votes on the amendment.

For substitution amendments specifically, Resolution No. 268 requires that the text be submitted in writing. In practice, it is good discipline to prepare all floor amendments in writing, regardless of type. A written amendment reduces confusion, provides a clear record, and ensures that the text the plenary votes on is the text the Member intended.

Practical Tips for Drafting Floor Amendments

Floor amendments are often drafted under pressure. A Member hears something during interpellation that prompts a concern. A provision that seemed clear in the committee report turns out to be ambiguous when read in the context of the full bill. A stakeholder contacts the Member’s office between sessions with a specific request.

Whatever the trigger, the drafter must produce amendment language quickly and accurately. Here are guidelines:

Get the exact text of the provision being amended. Before you draft anything, read the provision as it appears in the committee report version of the bill. Do not work from memory. Do not work from an earlier version. The text you are amending is the text in the committee report, as further modified by any committee amendments already adopted in plenary.

State the amendment with surgical precision. Identify the exact location — article, section, subsection, paragraph, sentence, line — where the change applies. State exactly what is being inserted, deleted, or substituted. Leave no room for confusion about which words are being changed.

Match the style of the bill. If the bill uses “shall,” your amendment uses “shall.” If the bill refers to the implementing agency as “the Ministry,” your amendment says “the Ministry” — not “the implementing agency” or “said Ministry.” Consistency is not optional. A floor amendment that introduces different terminology for the same concept creates an internal conflict in the bill.

Keep amendments focused. One amendment, one change. If you need to make three changes to three different sections, propose three separate amendments. Bundling multiple changes into a single amendment creates problems: the plenary may support one change but not the other two, and a bundled amendment forces an all-or-nothing vote.

Prepare a brief justification. When the Member presents the amendment, they will need to explain why. Prepare a one-paragraph justification that the Member can read or paraphrase. Keep it factual. State the problem, state the solution, and explain why the proposed text is better than the current text.

10.4 Drafting Amendment Language

This section provides the standard formats for drafting amendments in the Bangsamoro Parliament. Each format includes the exact phrasing you should use, followed by examples. These formats apply to both committee amendments and floor amendments, though the procedural context differs.

10.4.1 Insertion

An insertion amendment adds new language to a bill without changing any existing text. The new language could be a word, a phrase, a subsection, a section, or an entire article.

Inserting a Word or Phrase

When you are inserting one or more words into an existing provision, identify the exact location and specify the text to be inserted.

Format:

On page ☑, line [Y], after the word “[existing word],” insert the words “[new words].”

Example:

On page 4, line 12, after the word “consultation,” insert the words “with the affected communities.”

This would change a provision from:

…shall conduct a consultation before issuing the permit.

to:

…shall conduct a consultation with the affected communities before issuing the permit.

Inserting a Subsection

When you are adding a new subsection to an existing section, identify the section, specify where the new subsection goes, and provide the full text.

Format:

A new subsection (x) is hereby inserted in Section ☑ to read as follows:

“(x) [full text of new subsection].”

Example:

A new subsection (e) is hereby inserted in Section 8 to read as follows:

“(e) Coordination with Local Government Units. — The Ministry shall coordinate with the provincial and municipal governments within whose jurisdiction the project is to be implemented. No project shall commence without a written endorsement from the relevant local government unit.”

When inserting a subsection into an existing list, check whether the insertion requires renumbering of subsequent subsections. If Section 8 currently has subsections (a) through (d) and you are inserting a new (c), the existing (c) becomes (d) and the existing (d) becomes (e). Your amendment must account for this. State it explicitly:

A new subsection (c) is hereby inserted in Section 8, and the existing subsections (c) and (d) are accordingly redesignated as subsections (d) and (e), respectively.

Inserting a New Section

When you are adding an entirely new section, identify the location in the bill where it should appear.

Format:

A new Section ☑ is hereby inserted after Section [Y] to read as follows:

“SEC. ☑. [Section heading]. — [full text of new section].”

Example:

A new Section 15 is hereby inserted after Section 14 to read as follows:

“SEC. 15. Annual Report. — The Ministry shall submit an annual report to the Parliament, through the appropriate committee, on the implementation of this Act. The report shall include, at a minimum:

(a) The number of applications received, approved, and denied;

(b) The total amount of funds disbursed;

(c) A summary of compliance and enforcement actions taken; and

(d) Recommendations for legislative or administrative improvements.”

If the insertion of a new section requires renumbering of subsequent sections, state this clearly:

A new Section 15 is hereby inserted after Section 14, and Sections 15 through 22 of the bill are accordingly renumbered as Sections 16 through 23, respectively. All internal cross-references are adjusted accordingly.

That last phrase — “all internal cross-references are adjusted accordingly” — is important. If Section 18 of the original bill contains a reference to “Section 15,” and your amendment renumbers Section 15 to Section 16, the cross-reference must be updated. The amendment should note this. Better still, draft the amendment so that it avoids renumbering altogether — for example, by inserting “Section 14-A” instead of a new “Section 15.” The choice depends on the preference of the sponsor and the conventions of the Parliament.

Inserting a New Article

The same principles apply when inserting an entirely new article (a group of related sections). Use the following format:

A new Article ☑ is hereby inserted after Article [Y] to read as follows:

“ARTICLE ☑

[ARTICLE HEADING]

SEC. ☑. [Section heading]. — [text]

SEC. [X+1]. [Section heading]. — [text]”

10.4.2 Deletion

A deletion amendment removes existing language from a bill. This could be a word, a phrase, a clause, a subsection, a section, or an entire article.

Deleting a Word or Phrase

Format:

On page ☑, line [Y], delete the words “[words to be deleted].”

Example:

On page 6, line 3, delete the words “and their dependents.”

This would change a provision from:

…shall provide medical assistance to all qualified beneficiaries and their dependents.

to:

…shall provide medical assistance to all qualified beneficiaries.

Deleting a Subsection

Format:

Subsection (x) of Section ☑ is hereby deleted.

If deleting a subsection requires redesignation of subsequent subsections, state this:

Subsection (c) of Section 8 is hereby deleted, and subsections (d) and (e) are accordingly redesignated as subsections (c) and (d), respectively.

Example:

Subsection (b) of Section 5 is hereby deleted. The existing subsections (c), (d), and (e) are accordingly redesignated as subsections (b), (c), and (d), respectively.

Deleting an Entire Section

Format:

Section ☑ is hereby deleted.

Or, if the deletion is from an existing law rather than from the bill under consideration:

Section ☑ of [BAA No. XX], otherwise known as the “[short title],” is hereby repealed.

Example:

Section 12 of the bill is hereby deleted.

Sections 13 through 20 are accordingly renumbered as Sections 12 through 19, respectively. All internal cross-references are adjusted accordingly.

Note the distinction between “deleted” and “repealed.” You delete provisions from a bill that has not yet been enacted. You repeal provisions of a law that is already in force. The difference matters. An amendment to a pending bill deletes text. An amendatory bill — a bill that amends an existing BAA — repeals provisions. Use the correct term.

Deleting an Entire Article

Format:

Article ☑ is hereby deleted in its entirety. The subsequent articles are renumbered accordingly, and all internal cross-references are adjusted.

10.4.3 Substitution

A substitution amendment replaces existing language with new language. It combines a deletion and an insertion into a single operation.

Substituting a Word or Phrase

Format:

On page ☑, line [Y], the word “[old word]” is hereby amended to read “[new word].”

Or:

On page ☑, line [Y], delete the words “[old words]” and insert in lieu thereof the words “[new words].”

Example:

On page 3, line 8, the word “may” is hereby amended to read “shall.”

This changes a discretionary provision into a mandatory one — a common floor amendment. The drafter should be prepared to explain the legal effect of this change (discussed in Chapter 6).

Another example:

On page 7, line 15, delete the words “One Hundred Thousand Pesos (PhP100,000.00)” and insert in lieu thereof the words “Two Hundred Fifty Thousand Pesos (PhP250,000.00).”

Substituting a Subsection

Format:

Subsection (x) of Section ☑ is hereby amended to read as follows:

“(x) [full text of new subsection].”

Example:

Subsection (c) of Section 9 is hereby amended to read as follows:

“(c) Compliance Period. — The establishment shall comply with the standards prescribed under this Act within one hundred eighty (180) days from the effectivity of the implementing rules and regulations. The Ministry may grant a one-time extension of ninety (90) days upon written application showing good cause.”

Substituting an Entire Section

Format:

Section ☑ is hereby amended to read as follows:

“SEC. ☑. [Section heading]. — [full text of the amended section].”

Example:

Section 6 is hereby amended to read as follows:

“SEC. 6. Composition of the Board. — The Board shall be composed of seven (7) members, as follows:

(a) The Minister, or a duly designated representative, as Chairperson;

(b) Two (2) representatives from the Ministry of the Interior and Local Government;

(c) One (1) representative from the Ministry of Finance;

(d) One (1) representative from the private sector, nominated by the Bangsamoro Chamber of Commerce and Industry;

(e) One (1) representative from civil society, nominated by accredited civil society organizations in the Bangsamoro; and

(f) One (1) representative from the academe, nominated by public and private universities in the Bangsamoro.

The members under subsections (d), (e), and (f) shall be appointed by the Chief Minister upon the recommendation of the Board Chairperson.”

Substituting Language in an Existing Law (Amendatory Bills)

When a bill amends a section of an existing Bangsamoro Autonomy Act, the format is slightly different because you must identify the law being amended:

Format:

Section ☑ of [BAA No. XX], otherwise known as the “[short title],” is hereby amended to read as follows:

“SEC. ☑. [Section heading]. — [full text of the amended section].”

Example:

Section 66 of Bangsamoro Autonomy Act No. 18, otherwise known as the “Bangsamoro Education Code,” is hereby amended to read as follows:

“SEC. 66. Medium of Teaching and Learning in Basic Education. — For Kindergarten and Grade 1 to 3, the medium of teaching and learning shall be the mother tongue of the learners. Beginning Grade 4, the primary medium of instruction and learning shall be English, Filipino, and Arabic. The Ministry shall issue additional rules on the medium of instruction and learning in Basic Education, including the availability of qualified Arabic language teachers and appropriate instructional materials.”

When amending an existing law, always reproduce the entire section being amended, not just the changed portions. If you only show the changed words, readers must compare the amendment with the original law to understand the full provision as amended. Reproducing the entire section — with the changes incorporated — makes the amendment self-contained and reduces error.

Summary of Standard Phrases

Keep these phrases in your working vocabulary. They are the standard formulations for amendment language in the Bangsamoro Parliament:

Operation Standard Phrase
Insert words “after the word ‘☑,’ insert the words ‘[Y]’”
Insert subsection “A new subsection (x) is hereby inserted in Section ☑ to read as follows:”
Insert section “A new Section ☑ is hereby inserted after Section [Y] to read as follows:”
Delete words “delete the words ‘☑’”
Delete subsection “Subsection (x) of Section ☑ is hereby deleted”
Delete section “Section ☑ is hereby deleted”
Repeal existing law section “Section ☑ of [BAA No. XX] is hereby repealed”
Substitute words “the word ‘☑’ is hereby amended to read ‘[Y]’”
Substitute words (alternative) “delete the words ‘☑’ and insert in lieu thereof the words ‘[Y]’”
Substitute subsection “Subsection (x) of Section ☑ is hereby amended to read as follows:”
Substitute section “Section ☑ is hereby amended to read as follows:”
Amend existing law section “Section ☑ of [BAA No. XX] is hereby amended to read as follows:”
Renumber “Sections ☑ through [Y] are accordingly renumbered as Sections [X+1] through [Y+1]”

10.5 Substitute Bills

A substitute bill replaces the entire text of the original bill with a new version. It is the most sweeping form of amendment and raises particular procedural and drafting challenges.

When a Substitute Bill Is Appropriate

Substitute bills typically arise in one of these situations:

The committee stage produced fundamental changes. Sometimes the committee’s scrutiny reveals that the original bill’s approach is unworkable and a completely different structure is needed. Rather than offer dozens of individual amendments — which would be confusing and difficult to follow — the committee prepares a substitute bill that incorporates all changes into a clean, coherent text.

Multiple bills on the same subject need to be consolidated. When several Members file separate bills on the same topic, the committee may consolidate them into a single substitute bill that draws the best elements from each.

The bill was overtaken by events. New information, a change in policy direction, or a Supreme Court decision may require such extensive revision that starting over with a new text makes more practical sense than patching the original.

Negotiations among Members produce a compromise text. When competing factions in the Parliament hold different views on a bill, the negotiated compromise may take the form of a substitute bill that replaces the version reported out of committee.

Procedural Rules

Resolution No. 268 addresses substitute bills directly. Section 14 of Rule XVII states:

“A bill or resolution shall not be amended by substituting it with another which covers a subject distinct from that proposed in the original bill or resolution.”

This is the key constraint. A substitute bill must cover the same subject as the original. You cannot use the substitute bill mechanism to introduce an entirely new legislative measure that has nothing to do with the original bill. The germaneness requirement applies.

The substitute bill must go through the same approval process as any other amendment. If offered as a committee amendment, it is presented to the plenary as part of the committee report. If offered as a floor amendment, it must be submitted in writing — as required for all substitution amendments under Resolution No. 268 — and voted upon by the plenary.

Drafting a Substitute Bill

When you draft a substitute bill, you are essentially drafting a new bill that will take the place of the original. The substitute bill should:

Use a fresh numbering system. Start from Section 1. Do not try to maintain the numbering of the original bill if the structure has changed significantly. A clean numbering system signals to Members that this is a new text, not a patchwork of amendments to the old one.

Carry the same bill number. The substitute bill does not receive a new bill number. It carries the number of the original bill. This maintains the legislative record and avoids confusion about which bill is being acted on.

Include all provisions, not just the changed ones. A substitute bill must be a complete text. Members must be able to read it as a self-contained bill. Do not assume that readers will refer to the original version for unchanged provisions. If a provision from the original bill is retained in the substitute, reproduce it in full.

Explain the changes in the committee report. If the substitute bill originates from the committee, the committee report should explain why a substitute was necessary rather than individual amendments. A comparative matrix showing the differences between the original and the substitute is extremely helpful.

Preserve the long title or amend it. If the substitute bill changes the scope of the original bill, the long title must be amended to reflect the new scope. The one-subject rule still applies. Ensure that the long title accurately describes what the substitute bill does.

Format for Proposing a Substitute Bill

When a committee proposes a substitute bill, the committee report typically includes language such as:

The Committee on [name], after thorough deliberation, recommends the approval of the attached substitute bill in lieu of [Bill No. XX] filed by [author]. The substitute bill incorporates the committee’s amendments and consolidates provisions from [Bill No. XX], [Bill No. YY], and [Bill No. ZZ], which cover the same subject matter.

When a floor amendment proposes a substitute bill, the Member submits the complete text in writing and moves:

Mr./Madam Speaker, I move that a substitute bill be adopted in lieu of the present text of [Bill No. XX]. The substitute bill has been submitted in writing to the Secretary-General. The substitute covers the same subject matter as the original bill and incorporates amendments that address the concerns raised during the period of interpellation.

Drafting Tips for Substitute Bills

Start from the policy objectives, not from the original text. If the committee decided that a substitute is needed, it usually means the original bill’s approach was flawed at a structural level. Do not try to edit the original bill into the substitute. Start fresh with the policy objectives and build the new bill from the ground up.

Track the differences. Even though you are drafting a new text, you need to know how it differs from the original. Prepare a comparison document — a comparative matrix or a marked-up version of the original — that shows every change. This will be needed for the committee report and for the plenary debate.

Check the substitute for internal consistency. Because substitute bills are often drafted quickly under time pressure, they are prone to internal inconsistencies — a defined term that is not actually used in the body, a cross-reference to a section number that changed, a penalty provision that does not match the prohibitions. Read the substitute bill from beginning to end before finalizing it.

Do not forget the final provisions. When drafting a substitute in haste, it is easy to focus on the substantive provisions and forget the housekeeping: separability clause, repealing clause, effectivity clause. Check the final provisions against the standard bill structure (Chapter 3).

Amendments to Substitute Bills

Once the plenary adopts a substitute bill in lieu of the original, that substitute becomes the text under consideration. Further amendments — both committee amendments and floor amendments — are then made to the substitute bill’s text, not to the original bill. This seems obvious, but it is a common source of confusion. Once a substitute is adopted, the original bill no longer exists as a working text. All subsequent references should be to the substitute.

A Note on Amendatory Bills

This chapter has focused on amendments to pending bills — that is, bills that are still moving through the legislative process. But there is another category of amendment that the drafter encounters regularly: the amendatory bill. An amendatory bill is a new bill that proposes to amend an existing, enacted Bangsamoro Autonomy Act.

Amendatory bills follow the full legislative process. They are filed, read, referred to committee, scrutinized, debated, and voted on just like any other bill. The difference is in their content. Instead of establishing new law, they modify law that already exists.

The drafting conventions for amendatory bills include:

The long title must identify the law being amended. This is required by Resolution No. 268, Section 7(g) of Rule XVII: “If a bill proposes to amend or revise an existing law, the title must mention the law being amended or revised.”

Example:

AN ACT AMENDING SECTIONS 5, 12, AND 18 OF BANGSAMORO AUTONOMY ACT NO. 29, OTHERWISE KNOWN AS THE “BANGSAMORO CIVIL SERVICE CODE,” AND FOR OTHER PURPOSES

Each amended section must be reproduced in full. When you amend a section of an existing law, do not show only the changed words. Reproduce the entire section as it will read after the amendment takes effect. This is the standard practice in the Bangsamoro Parliament and in Philippine legislation generally.

Use the standard amendatory clause. The body of the bill uses the format described in Section 10.4.3:

Section ☑ of Bangsamoro Autonomy Act No. [XX], otherwise known as the “[short title],” is hereby amended to read as follows:

Include a repealing clause if necessary. If the amendatory bill deletes entire sections or provisions of the existing law, include a specific repealing clause:

Section ☑ of Bangsamoro Autonomy Act No. [XX] is hereby repealed.

Do not use general repealing clauses (“all laws, decrees, orders, or parts thereof inconsistent with this Act are hereby repealed or modified accordingly”) as a substitute for identifying the specific provisions being repealed. General repealing clauses create uncertainty about what survives and what does not. Be specific.

Summary

Amendments are the Parliament’s tool for refining legislation. Whether offered by a committee after weeks of scrutiny or by a Member during a floor debate, every amendment follows the same basic structure: it identifies a specific part of the text, states the change to be made, and provides the new language.

The drafter’s job is to make that change cleanly and precisely. Match the style of the bill. Maintain the numbering. Check the cross-references. And always — always — put the amendment in writing before it is moved on the floor. The parliamentary record depends on it. The quality of the resulting law depends on it. The people who must live under that law depend on it.

Back to Table of Contents

Chapter 11 — Quality Review

Drafting a bill is only half the work. The other half is making sure it holds up.

A bill that seems clear on the day you finish drafting it may turn out to be ambiguous when a committee member reads it under the pressure of a hearing. A provision that covers every scenario you imagined may fail to cover the one scenario nobody thought of. A penalty clause that seems proportionate on paper may be unconstitutional when measured against the BOL’s human rights provisions. These are not hypothetical problems. They happen. They happen in every legislature, and they happen in the Bangsamoro Parliament.

Quality review is how you catch these problems before the Parliament votes. It is the systematic process of reading your own work with fresh eyes — or, better, with hostile eyes — and asking whether the bill does what it is supposed to do, says what it is supposed to say, and survives the scrutiny that will come from committees, the floor, implementing agencies, courts, and the public.

This chapter gives you the tools for that process. It begins with a comprehensive self-review checklist. It introduces the interpellation test — a practical exercise that forces you to read your bill the way an opposition Member would. It catalogs the most common drafting mistakes, with examples and corrections. And it addresses specialized reviews that every Bangsamoro bill should undergo: gender sensitivity, human rights compliance, and BOL conformity.

11.1 The Drafter’s Self-Review Checklist

Before you submit a draft to anyone — before the MP sees it, before it goes to co-authors, before it is filed — run through this checklist. It is organized into four categories: legal authority, structure and completeness, language and style, and internal consistency. Each item is a question. If the answer is “no” or “I’m not sure,” stop and fix the problem.

This checklist can also serve as the basis for Appendix E of this manual — a detachable quality review checklist for daily use.

11.1.1 Legal Authority

These items verify that the bill is within the Parliament’s power and does not conflict with superior law.

  1. Does the bill fall within the enumerated powers of the Bangsamoro Government under Article V, Section 2 of the BOL?
  2. If the bill relies on the general welfare clause (Article V, Section 3), is there a reasonable connection between the bill’s subject and the welfare of the Bangsamoro people?
  3. Does the bill conflict with any provision of the 1987 Constitution?
  4. Does the bill conflict with any provision of the BOL itself?
  5. Does the bill intrude on powers reserved to Congress under the Constitution or the BOL (the residual clause)?
  6. Does the bill conflict with any national law that applies in the Bangsamoro?
  7. Have you checked whether any Supreme Court decisions affect the bill’s subject matter?
  8. If the bill imposes taxes or fees, does it comply with the fiscal provisions of the BOL (Article XII)?
  9. If the bill creates a government body, does it comply with the BOL provisions on bureaucratic structure?
  10. If the bill deals with personal, family, or property relations involving Muslims, does it respect the jurisdiction of the Shari’ah courts?

11.1.2 Structure and Completeness

These items verify that the bill contains all necessary parts and that nothing is missing.

  1. Does the bill have a long title that accurately describes its content and scope?
  2. Does the long title comply with the one-subject, one-title rule?
  3. Does the bill include the proper enacting clause? The standard form is “Be it enacted by the Bangsamoro Parliament:” — or, during the BTA period, “Be it enacted by the Bangsamoro Transition Authority Parliament in session assembled:”
  4. Does the bill include a short title?
  5. Does the bill include a declaration of policy?
  6. Does the bill include a definition of terms section, and are all terms used in a technical or specialized sense defined?
  7. Does the definition section avoid defining words in their ordinary meaning?
  8. Does the bill include a scope and coverage section that makes clear who and what the bill applies to?
  9. Are the substantive provisions organized in a logical sequence — from general to specific, from rights to obligations, from prohibitions to penalties?
  10. Does every duty or obligation in the bill identify who bears that duty?
  11. Does every right created by the bill identify who may exercise it and who must respect it?
  12. Does every prohibition have a corresponding penalty or enforcement mechanism?
  13. Does the bill identify the implementing agency or agencies?
  14. Does the bill include provisions for implementing rules and regulations (IRR) if the subject matter requires detailed rulemaking?
  15. Does the IRR provision include a timeline for promulgation?
  16. Does the bill include an appropriation or funding provision, or does it state that funding shall come from the annual Bangsamoro Expenditure Program?
  17. Does the bill include a separability clause?
  18. Does the bill include a repealing clause that specifically identifies provisions of existing law that are being repealed or modified?
  19. Does the bill include an effectivity clause?
  20. Does the bill include transitory provisions if they are needed — for example, to address existing conditions, pending applications, or the transition from the old regime to the new one?

11.1.3 Language and Style

These items verify that the bill is written clearly and follows the conventions of Bangsamoro legislative drafting.

  1. Is every sentence in the active voice where possible?
  2. Is the bill written in the present tense?
  3. Does the bill use “shall” for mandatory duties, “may” for permissive authority, and “must” for conditions?
  4. Are the uses of “shall,” “may,” and “must” consistent throughout?
  5. Does any sentence exceed four lines of text? If so, can it be broken into shorter sentences or a numbered list?
  6. Does the bill avoid archaic legalisms (“hereinbefore,” “hereinafter,” “aforementioned,” “whereas” in the body)?
  7. Does the bill avoid redundant doublets (“null and void,” “cease and desist,” “each and every”)?
  8. Are numbers written in both words and figures — “thirty (30) days” — as is the convention?
  9. Are monetary amounts written in both words and figures with the abbreviation PhP?
  10. Does the bill use a defined term consistently — the same term every time, never a synonym?
  11. Are cross-references accurate? Does every reference to “Section X” actually point to the correct section?
  12. Are lists formatted correctly — with consistent punctuation, consistent numbering, and proper use of “and” or “or” before the final item?
  13. Does the bill avoid vague phrases like “as may be necessary,” “within a reasonable time,” or “to the extent practicable” without further specificity?

11.1.4 Internal Consistency

These items verify that the bill does not contradict itself.

  1. Does the bill use the same term for the same concept throughout? (Not “Ministry” in one section and “Department” in another for the same agency.)
  2. If the bill refers to another law by short title, is the short title correct?
  3. If the bill quotes the BOL, is the quotation verbatim?
  4. Are the section numbers sequential with no gaps or duplicates?
  5. Does the table of contents (if included) match the actual section headings?
  6. If the bill lists qualifications for an office or position, are the same qualifications repeated consistently in all relevant provisions — or are they stated once and cross-referenced?
  7. Does the penalty provision cover all prohibited acts defined in the bill? Are any prohibited acts missing a penalty?
  8. Does the bill’s coverage match its declaration of policy? (If the declaration says the bill applies to “all persons,” does the substantive provision also apply to “all persons” — or does it narrow the scope without explanation?)
  9. Are all timelines internally consistent? (Does the bill give an agency 30 days to process an application in one section and 60 days in another for the same application?)
  10. If the bill creates a board or committee, does it specify the same number of members in the composition section as in the quorum and voting provisions?

11.2 The Interpellation Test

The self-review checklist catches technical problems. The interpellation test catches substantive ones.

Here is the exercise. Put down the draft. Walk away from it for an hour, or a day if you have the time. Then sit down and read the bill as if you were a Member of Parliament who is skeptical of it. Not hostile — skeptical. You are a thoughtful MP who wants to understand what the bill does, whether it will work, and what it will cost. You are looking for weaknesses, not because you want the bill to fail, but because you want to make it better before it reaches the floor.

Ask yourself these questions about every major provision:

Policy Questions

  • What problem does this bill solve? Is the problem real, documented, and significant enough to justify legislation?
  • Has the Parliament already addressed this problem through an existing BAA? If so, why is a new bill needed instead of amending the existing law?
  • Who benefits from this bill? Who bears the costs?
  • Will the bill actually achieve its stated objective? What could go wrong?
  • Are there unintended consequences? Will the bill create new problems while solving old ones?
  • Is this bill the right tool? Could the same objective be achieved through an executive order, an administrative regulation, or an appropriation?

Legal Questions

  • Where does the Parliament get the power to legislate on this subject? Can you point to the specific BOL provision?
  • Does this bill conflict with any existing BAA? If so, does the repealing clause address the conflict?
  • Could a constitutional challenge be raised against any provision? On what grounds?
  • Does the bill delegate too much authority to the executive? Does it set sufficient standards to guide the exercise of that delegated authority?

Implementation Questions

  • Which agency will implement this bill? Does that agency have the capacity — staff, budget, expertise — to do so?
  • How much will implementation cost? Where will the money come from?
  • Does the bill require the creation of new offices, positions, or systems? If so, is there funding for them?
  • What happens if the implementing agency does not issue the required IRR within the prescribed timeline? Does the bill have a fallback mechanism?
  • How will compliance be monitored? Who checks whether the law is being followed?

Enforcement Questions

  • If someone violates this bill, who detects the violation?
  • Who investigates? Who prosecutes?
  • Are the penalties proportionate to the offense? Are they high enough to deter violations? Are they so high that they might be struck down as excessive?
  • Can a small business owner, a farmer, or a fisherfolk reasonably be expected to understand and comply with this bill?

Drafting Questions

  • Is there a provision in this bill that a reasonable person could read two different ways? If so, which reading did the drafter intend, and can the ambiguity be resolved by revising the text?
  • Is there a gap — a scenario that should be covered but is not?
  • Is there a contradiction between two provisions?
  • Does the bill say anything that is purely aspirational and cannot be enforced? If so, does it belong in the bill, or should it be in the explanatory note?

The Test in Practice

The interpellation test is not theoretical. It simulates what will actually happen when the bill reaches the plenary. A Member will stand up and ask these exact questions. If your bill cannot withstand them, it is not ready for filing.

Sit down with the sponsoring MP and run through the questions. Or assign a colleague to play the role of the interpellator. The goal is to identify every soft spot in the bill before it reaches the committee report stage. Fix the soft spots. Strengthen the weak provisions. Add what is missing. Cut what is indefensible.

A bill that survives the interpellation test is a bill that will survive the plenary.

11.3 Common Drafting Mistakes

Below are specific mistakes that appear repeatedly in legislative drafts — not just in the Bangsamoro Parliament, but in legislatures everywhere. Each entry states the mistake, gives an example, and shows how to fix it.

1. Unfunded mandate. The bill creates obligations for an agency without appropriating funds or identifying a funding source.

Problem: “The Ministry of Health shall establish a primary care clinic in every municipality.”

Fix: Add an appropriation section: “The amount necessary for the implementation of this Act shall be included in the annual Bangsamoro Expenditure Program. For the first year of implementation, the amount of ☑ is hereby appropriated from [source].”

2. Missing implementing agency. The bill says something must be done but does not say who does it.

Problem: “A comprehensive inventory of all agricultural lands shall be conducted within one (1) year.”

Fix: “The Ministry of Agriculture, Fisheries and Agrarian Reform shall conduct a comprehensive inventory of all agricultural lands within one (1) year from the effectivity of this Act.”

3. Penalty without prohibition. The bill penalizes an act that is not actually prohibited anywhere in the bill’s substantive provisions.

Problem: The penal section says “Any person who violates Section 8 of this Act shall be fined…” but Section 8 only describes how the permit application process works. It does not prohibit anything.

Fix: Either add a clear prohibition (“No person shall operate a [facility] without a valid permit issued under Section 8 of this Act”) or remove the penalty reference to Section 8.

4. Prohibition without penalty. The bill prohibits an act but prescribes no consequence for violation.

Problem: “No person shall discharge industrial waste into waterways within the Bangsamoro.” The bill says nothing more — no fine, no imprisonment, no administrative sanction.

Fix: Add a penalty provision. Or, if the intention is that existing penal laws apply, state this explicitly: “Violations of this section shall be subject to the penalties prescribed under [specific law].”

5. Vague timeline. The bill sets a deadline using imprecise language.

Problem: “The implementing rules and regulations shall be promulgated within a reasonable time after the effectivity of this Act.”

Fix: “The implementing rules and regulations shall be promulgated within ninety (90) days from the effectivity of this Act.”

6. Undefined technical term. The bill uses a specialized term without defining it, leaving its meaning open to dispute.

Problem: A bill on environmental protection refers to “sustainable practices” throughout but never defines what qualifies as sustainable.

Fix: Add a definition: “‘Sustainable practices’ refers to methods of resource use, production, or development that meet present needs without compromising the ability of future generations to meet their own needs, as determined by the standards prescribed in the implementing rules and regulations.”

7. Inconsistent terminology. The bill uses different words for the same thing.

Problem: Section 3 refers to the “Board of Directors.” Section 7 refers to the “Governing Board.” Section 12 refers to the “Board.” All three mean the same body.

Fix: Choose one term — “the Board” — define it in the definitions section, and use it consistently throughout.

8. Conflicting provisions. Two sections of the same bill contradict each other.

Problem: Section 5 says the permit is valid for three (3) years. Section 14 says the permit must be renewed annually.

Fix: Determine which is correct and amend the other. If both are partly correct (e.g., the permit is valid for three years but requires annual compliance reporting), draft both provisions to reflect the actual intent clearly.

9. Dangling cross-reference. A provision refers to a section number that does not exist or that refers to the wrong provision because sections were renumbered during revision.

Problem: “The exemptions provided under Section 14 of this Act shall not apply to…” But the bill only has 12 sections. Section 14 was deleted during revision, and the cross-reference was never updated.

Fix: Run a systematic check of every cross-reference in the bill before finalizing. Match each reference to its target.

10. Overbroad delegation. The bill gives an agency open-ended authority without sufficient standards.

Problem: “The Ministry shall have the power to issue rules and regulations to carry out the purposes of this Act, including the power to impose fees and penalties as it may deem necessary.”

Fix: Specify limits. “The Ministry shall have the power to issue rules and regulations to carry out the purposes of this Act: Provided, That any fees imposed under such rules shall not exceed [amount or formula], and any administrative penalties shall not exceed [amount or sanction], subject to the requirements of due process.”

11. Redundant provision. The bill restates something that is already in the BOL or an existing BAA, adding nothing.

Problem: “The Members of the Parliament shall enjoy parliamentary immunity as provided by law.” This adds nothing beyond what the BOL already provides.

Fix: Remove the provision. If the drafter wants to call attention to the existing immunity provision for context, do so in the explanatory note, not in the body of the bill.

12. Aspirational provision masquerading as law. The bill includes a provision that expresses a goal but creates no rights, duties, or enforceable obligations.

Problem: “The Bangsamoro Government shall endeavor to promote the holistic well-being of youth in the region.”

Fix: Either remove the provision or convert it into an enforceable obligation: “The Ministry of Social Services and Development shall establish a youth development program in every province and city within the Bangsamoro. The program shall include, at a minimum: (a)… (b)… (c)…”

13. Missing transition provisions. The bill creates a new regime but does not address what happens to existing arrangements.

Problem: A bill creates a new licensing system for a regulated industry but says nothing about businesses that already hold licenses under the old system.

Fix: Add a transitory provision: “All licenses issued under [prior law] that are valid on the date of effectivity of this Act shall remain in force until their expiration. Licensees shall apply for renewal under this Act at least sixty (60) days before the expiration of their existing licenses.”

14. Gender-exclusive language. The bill uses masculine pronouns as the default.

Problem: “The Chairperson shall submit his report within thirty (30) days.”

Fix: “The Chairperson shall submit a report within thirty (30) days.” Or, if a pronoun is needed: “The Chairperson shall submit his or her report within thirty (30) days.”

15. Using “and/or.” This construction is ambiguous. Does it mean both conditions must be met, or either one?

Problem: “A fine and/or imprisonment shall be imposed.”

Fix: Decide what you mean. If you mean both: “A fine and imprisonment shall be imposed.” If you mean either or both: “A fine, imprisonment, or both, shall be imposed.”

16. Passive voice obscuring responsibility. The bill says something must be done but uses the passive voice, leaving unclear who is responsible.

Problem: “The necessary funds shall be allocated.”

Fix: “The Ministry of Finance, Budget and Management shall allocate the necessary funds.”

17. No sunset clause for a pilot program. The bill establishes a temporary or experimental program but does not include an expiration date.

Problem: A “pilot program” is created with no stated end date, no evaluation mechanism, and no trigger for expansion or termination.

Fix: Add a sunset clause: “This program shall operate for a period of three (3) years from the effectivity of this Act. Within six (6) months before the expiration of this period, the implementing agency shall submit to the Parliament, through the appropriate committee, an evaluation report on the program’s effectiveness. The Parliament may extend the program upon review of the evaluation.”

18. Quorum and voting provisions that do not add up. The bill creates a body and specifies a quorum or voting threshold that is mathematically problematic.

Problem: A board has seven (7) members, and the bill requires a “two-thirds vote of all members” for certain decisions. Two-thirds of seven is 4.67 — not a whole number.

Fix: “A vote of at least five (5) members shall be required for…” Or specify: “a vote of two-thirds of all members, rounded up to the nearest whole number.”

19. General repealing clause with no specific repeals. The bill ends with “All laws, decrees, orders, rules and regulations, or parts thereof, inconsistent with the provisions of this Act are hereby repealed or modified accordingly” but does not identify any specific law being repealed.

Problem: Nobody — not the implementing agency, not the courts, not the public — knows what has been repealed.

Fix: Identify specific provisions: “Section 12 of BAA No. 5 and Section 8(c) of BAA No. 19 are hereby repealed.” If a general repealing clause is also desired, place it after the specific repeals as a safety net — but never as a substitute.

20. Incomplete effectivity clause. The bill omits or truncates the effectivity clause.

Problem: “This Act shall take effect upon its approval.” But under the BOL and the Constitution, laws require publication before taking effect.

Fix: “This Act shall take effect fifteen (15) days following its complete publication in at least one (1) newspaper of general circulation within the Bangsamoro Autonomous Region in Muslim Mindanao.”

11.4 Gender Sensitivity Review

Every bill filed in the Bangsamoro Parliament should be reviewed for gender sensitivity. This is not a matter of political correctness. It is a legal obligation rooted in the BOL, the Bangsamoro Development Plan, and the Philippines’ commitments under international law, including the Convention on the Elimination of All Forms of Discrimination Against Women (CEDAW).

Article IX, Section 11 of the BOL requires that “there shall be at least one (1) woman to be appointed to the Bangsamoro Cabinet” and that the Parliament shall enact a law “ensuring the representation of women in other decision-making and policy-determining bodies of the Bangsamoro Government.” Article XIII, Section 5 further provides that the Bangsamoro Government “shall guarantee full and direct participation of women in governance and development process, ensuring that women shall benefit equally in the implementation of development programs and projects.” The Bangsamoro Development Plan 2023-2028 identifies gender mainstreaming as a cross-cutting strategy across all sectors. These commitments must be reflected in legislation.

What a Gender Sensitivity Review Covers

A gender sensitivity review examines the bill at three levels:

Language. Does the bill use gender-neutral or gender-inclusive language? The standard in modern legislative drafting is to avoid masculine pronouns as the default. Instead of “he” or “his,” use “the person,” “the official,” or “he or she” and “his or her.” Instead of “chairman,” use “chairperson.” Instead of “fireman,” use “firefighter.” The goal is not to police vocabulary but to ensure that the law, as written, treats women and men equally in its language.

Where the bill addresses a role that has historically been associated with one gender, consider whether the language inadvertently excludes the other. A bill on “fishermen’s cooperatives” may unintentionally signal that women who fish are not covered. Changing the term to “fisherfolk cooperatives” resolves the issue without changing the substance.

Substance. Does the bill affect women and men differently? If so, does it account for those differences? Consider:

  • Does the bill provide for maternity protections if it regulates employment?
  • Does the bill ensure women’s representation on boards, committees, and decision-making bodies it creates?
  • If the bill addresses land rights, does it recognize the property rights of women, including married women?
  • If the bill establishes benefit programs, are the eligibility criteria designed in a way that women can realistically meet?
  • Does the bill address gender-based violence if relevant to its subject matter?
  • Does the bill include sex-disaggregated data collection requirements for monitoring and evaluation?

Impact. Will the bill have a disproportionate impact on women, even if its language is facially neutral? A bill that requires applicants to appear in person at a provincial office during business hours may disproportionately burden women who have caregiving responsibilities. A bill that requires collateral for a loan program may disproportionately exclude women who do not hold property titles in their own names.

The Gender Review Checklist

Apply these specific checks to every bill:

  1. Does the bill use gender-neutral language throughout?
  2. Does the bill provide for women’s representation in any body it creates?
  3. If the bill regulates employment, does it include maternity and parental leave protections?
  4. Does the bill require sex-disaggregated data collection for monitoring purposes?
  5. Does the bill allocate a portion of its budget to gender and development (GAD) programs, consistent with existing GAD budget requirements?
  6. Does the bill contain provisions that could disproportionately disadvantage women, even if facially neutral?
  7. Have women’s organizations and gender experts been consulted during the drafting process?
  8. Does the bill align with the Bangsamoro Development Plan’s gender mainstreaming strategy?

11.5 Human Rights Review

The BOL contains a comprehensive set of rights provisions in Article IX (Basic Rights) that go beyond restating the rights in the Philippine Constitution. The 1987 Constitution’s own Bill of Rights (Article III of the Constitution) applies to all persons in the Philippines, including everyone within the Bangsamoro. The BOL’s Article IX adds Bangsamoro-specific rights — transitional justice, reparation for unjust dispossession, indigenous peoples’ rights, customary rights, religious freedom, human rights protections, labor rights, women’s protection, and the rights of settlers and non-Moro indigenous communities.

Every bill must be reviewed against these provisions. A bill that violates human rights — even unintentionally — is a bill that invites litigation, invalidation, and public opposition.

The Human Rights Review Process

Step 1: Identify the rights at stake. For every bill, ask: Which fundamental rights does this bill touch? A bill regulating assembly may implicate freedom of assembly. A bill establishing a regulatory body with investigative powers may implicate the right against unreasonable searches. A bill on social media may implicate freedom of expression. Identify all affected rights.

Step 2: Check the BOL Basic Rights and the Constitution’s Bill of Rights. Read Article IX (Basic Rights) of the BOL and Article III (Bill of Rights) of the 1987 Constitution, and determine whether any provision of the bill conflicts with any right recognized there. Pay particular attention to:

  • The right to due process and equal protection (1987 Constitution, Article III, Sections 1-2)
  • The right to freedom of expression, assembly, and association (Constitution, Article III)
  • The right against unreasonable searches and seizures (Constitution, Article III)
  • The rights of the accused in criminal proceedings (Constitution, Article III)
  • The right to privacy (Constitution, Article III)
  • The rights of indigenous peoples within the Bangsamoro (BOL, Article IX, Section 3)
  • The rights of women (BOL, Article IX, Sections 11-12)
  • Customary rights and non-discrimination (BOL, Article IX, Section 4)

Step 3: Check the bill’s penalty provisions. If the bill imposes criminal penalties, verify that:

  • The prohibited acts are described with sufficient specificity (no vague or overbroad prohibitions)
  • The penalties are proportionate to the offense
  • Due process protections are observed (notice, hearing, right to counsel)
  • The bill does not impose retroactive penalties
  • The bill does not create a penalty so severe that it amounts to cruel or unusual punishment

Step 4: Check administrative provisions for due process. If the bill creates administrative proceedings — license revocation, disciplinary proceedings, permit cancellation — verify that the affected person has the right to:

  • Receive written notice of the charges or grounds
  • Present evidence and witnesses
  • Be represented by counsel
  • Receive a written decision stating the reasons
  • Appeal the decision to a higher authority or to a court

Step 5: Check the bill against international obligations. The Philippines is a signatory to numerous international human rights instruments that are binding in the Bangsamoro. Check the bill against:

  • The International Covenant on Civil and Political Rights (ICCPR)
  • The International Covenant on Economic, Social and Cultural Rights (ICESCR)
  • The Convention on the Elimination of All Forms of Discrimination Against Women (CEDAW)
  • The Convention on the Rights of the Child (CRC)
  • The United Nations Declaration on the Rights of Indigenous Peoples (UNDRIP)
  • The Comprehensive Agreement on the Bangsamoro (CAB), to the extent its provisions inform the interpretation of BOL rights

Common Human Rights Issues in Legislation

  • Licensing or permit requirements that restrict the exercise of fundamental rights (e.g., requiring a permit for peaceful assembly)
  • Broad investigative powers without judicial oversight
  • Administrative detention provisions that bypass the courts
  • Provisions that discriminate — directly or indirectly — based on ethnicity, religion, sex, or other protected categories
  • Penal provisions that lack mens rea (intent) requirements for serious offenses
  • Collective punishment provisions (penalizing a community for the acts of individuals)

11.6 BOL Compliance Review

The Bangsamoro Organic Law is the supreme law of the Bangsamoro Autonomous Region, subordinate only to the Philippine Constitution. Every bill enacted by the Parliament must conform to the BOL. A bill that conflicts with the BOL is ultra vires — beyond the Parliament’s power — and may be struck down by the courts.

The BOL compliance review is a systematic check of the bill against the relevant provisions of the BOL. It is more specific than the general legal authority check in the self-review checklist (Section 11.1.1). It requires you to read the BOL with the bill beside you and compare them, provision by provision.

How to Conduct a BOL Compliance Review

Step 1: Identify the relevant BOL provisions. Read the bill and list every BOL article that touches on the bill’s subject matter. For most bills, this includes:

  • Article V (Powers of Government) — Does the bill fall within the enumerated or general welfare powers?
  • Article XII (Fiscal Autonomy) — Does the bill involve taxation, revenue, or appropriation?
  • Article IX (Basic Rights) — Does the bill affect fundamental rights, including transitional justice, indigenous peoples, women, labor, or human rights?
  • Article VII (Parliament) — Does the bill comply with the rules on legislative procedure?
  • Article X (Bangsamoro Justice System) — Does the bill touch on Shari’ah jurisdiction or court procedures?

Step 2: Check for direct conflicts. Read each relevant BOL provision and ask: Does anything in this bill directly contradict what the BOL says? Examples:

  • Article XII, Section 9 of the BOL provides that the taxing power of the Bangsamoro Government “shall not extend to” income taxes (except on banks and financial institutions), customs duties, and several other categories. A bill imposing a regional income tax would directly conflict with this provision.
  • Article IX, Section 11 of the BOL requires the Parliament to enact a law “ensuring the representation of women in other decision-making and policy-determining bodies.” A bill that creates a governing body with no provision for women’s representation may conflict with this requirement.
  • The BOL requires that laws take effect after publication. A bill with an effectivity clause that bypasses publication conflicts with the BOL.

Step 3: Check for implicit conflicts. Some conflicts are not obvious on the surface. They arise when the bill’s provisions, taken as a whole, undermine the spirit or intent of the BOL even if no single provision directly contradicts a specific section. Examples:

  • A bill that concentrates power in a single agency to such a degree that it effectively undermines the decentralization principle embedded in the BOL
  • A bill that creates a regulatory framework so burdensome that it effectively nullifies a right recognized in the BOL
  • A bill that delegates so much authority to the executive that it effectively transfers legislative power away from the Parliament

Step 4: Check boundary issues. The BOL divides powers between the Bangsamoro Government and the national government. Some powers are exclusive to the Bangsamoro. Others are concurrent. Still others are reserved to Congress. The boundary is not always sharp. For bills that operate near this boundary, ask:

  • Is this subject among the fifty-five enumerated powers in Article V, Section 2?
  • If not, does the general welfare clause apply?
  • Is there a national law on the same subject? If so, does the BOL give the Parliament authority to legislate on the subject despite the national law?
  • Has the Supreme Court issued any ruling on the scope of Bangsamoro authority over this subject?

Step 5: Document your findings. Prepare a brief BOL compliance memo — even if it is just a one-page summary — stating which BOL provisions you reviewed, your conclusion, and any areas of concern. This memo serves as an internal record and can be shared with the sponsoring MP or the committee.

11.7 Bangsamoro Parliamentary Conventions

Every legislature develops practices and conventions that are not written in the formal rules but are understood by experienced Members and staff. The Bangsamoro Parliament, though young, has already established several such conventions. Knowing them will help you produce drafts that align with how the Parliament actually works, not just how the rules say it should work.

Conventions on Bill Drafting and Format

The explanatory note is expected but not required. Resolution No. 268 describes the explanatory statement as “optional” (Rule XVII, Section 7(a)). In practice, every bill filed in the Parliament includes one. The explanatory note has become a de facto requirement. Omitting it invites questions about the bill’s purpose and weakens the sponsoring MP’s position during deliberation.

The committee report accompanies every bill reported out. Section 16 of Rule XII requires committees to prepare a report containing discussions, views, amendments, and justifications. In practice, the committee report has evolved into a substantial document — sometimes longer than the bill itself — that serves as the primary reference during plenary deliberation. Drafters should anticipate contributing to the committee report by preparing amendment summaries and justifications.

Government bills take precedence. Resolution No. 268 provides that government bills have precedence over private member bills “without prejudice to consolidation.” In practice, the legislative calendar is driven by the government’s priority agenda. Drafters working on government bills should be prepared for accelerated timelines. Drafters working on private member bills should plan for the possibility that the bill may wait in the calendar for a longer period.

Consolidation is preferred over competition.
